He also collected numerous materials on the Eagle squadrons, the American pilots who flew for the British during WWII.
This series of articles is divided into two subseries: Vern Haugland and Other Authors. Most of the clippings by Haugland are written on the NASA space program, and center on the Apollo missions, missile testing, and army rocket tests. People that Haugland wrote about were Charles Lindbergh, General Douglas MacArthur, and Admiral Richard Byrd. Other topics that he was interested dealt with aviation, including air travel, airports, and different types of aircrafts. The articles written by other authors that Haugland collected dealt with the NASA space program, missile testing, and satellites. Other topics included moon research, the Eagle Squadron, and the Air Force. Articles written about the Haugland family and Haugland himself are also in the series. Many of these articles are about Haugland's time in New Guinea and the book that Haugland wrote about his experiences, his war years, and copies of his obituaries after his death in 1984.
This series consists of booklets and pamphlets from many of Haugland's memberships, including the Aviation Writers Association and the National Aeronautic Association. His work journals are also included in the series, and many are written in shorthand. Haugland kept travel receipts and memorabilia from the Pacific island and Soviet Union trips that he took while working for the Associated Press. He received many certificates and citations, such as honorary memberships from the many ships that he traveled on while covering the NASA splashdowns and awards from writing associations. Correspondence with his wife, Tess, and his daughters is also in the series. There is also biographical information on Haugland in the series. A WWII based novel is also included within the series. This book deals with the war's 100 most fascinating stories. Haugland's story about his ordeal in New Guinea is included in the book.
This series contains artifacts that Haugland collected during his years with the Associated Press. Many of the artifacts relate to aviation, such as a wing remnant from the New Orleans first round-the-world flight in 1924 and a plaque commemorating the first flight by the Wright brothers in 1903. Other memorabilia centers on the NASA space program, mainly the Apollo missions. Also in the series are artifacts dedicated to Haugland on the numerous awards that he received. He received the Associated Press Medal of Honor, the Associated Press Strebig-Dobben Memorial Award, the Air Force Association Arts and Letter Award. Haugland also received an airplane propeller in 1973 signed by his friends in aviation.
This series consists of photographs of Haugland and his career, and of photographs he collected in the course of his work. The latter mostly relate to the military and aviation.
This series includes twelve aircraft lithographs presented to Haugland as a gift from Northrop Aircraft Inc. at an unknown date.
This photocopied collection is composed of a newspaper clipping dated July 15, 1865, Franklin Miller's diary from January 1-August 5, 1867, and his 1866 identification card at Fort Riley, Kansas.
The newspaper clipping is from a Soldier's Letter, Second Colorado Calvary of Fort Riley, Kansas. It describes Indian attacks, battles, deaths, and other news, military and general.
Franklin Miller's diary begins January 1, 1867, and contains accounts of the weather, the regiment, deaths of men in the company, burials, correspondence with family and friends, company inspections, deserting soldiers, arrival of recruits, and Miller's discharge from Camp Cooke on August 5, 1867. The diary also records the passing of several steamer ships and contains two poems written by Franklin Miller.
This collection contains correspondence, financial, legal, promotional, and organizational records generated by the Collins Land Company as well as government publications regarding federal public lands and business records from three other Collins family enterprises. The Collins Land Company was based in Helena, Montana, and bought and sold public lands. Materials in this collection provide particular insights into the history, procedures, and economy of public land management and sale during the first half of the twentieth century.
This series contains incoming, outgoing, interoffice and general correspondence generated and collected by the Collins Land Company detailing business projects, proposals, and practices spanning the first four decades of company operation. Series materials are almost exclusively exchanges with clients, potential buyers, and Federal land management officials. These records provide insights into the procedures and economy of public land sales during the first half of the 20th Century.
This series contains land scrip case histories collected by the Collins Land Company as documentation for title purchases and transfers. The Collins Land Company created four distinct sequences for these case histories and attached a case number to each file (beginning at zero for each sequence). The original sequences and case numbers have been retained for historical reference; however, records explaining the tracking system rationale and detailing gaps in each sequence no longer existed at the time the collection was accessioned to the archives. These records provide early property definitions and ownership histories for land parcels across the United States, with particular emphasis on Montana and the Pacific Northwest.
This series contains one folder of financial records, 10 bound financial volumes, and one folder of legal documents generated by the Collins Land Company, including two volumes tracking account activity for land scrips allotted to Union Civil War veterans and soldiers who fought in the Spanish-American War. Legal documents include property deeds, prospecting patents, and court transcripts.
This series contains Collins Land Company publications and advertisements as well as an extensive sequence of Federal government publications relating to management and dissolution of public lands. These government publications include Congressional legislation and U.S. Department of the Interior regulations.
This series consists of one bound volume containing the Collins Land Company's corporate bylaws and stockholder meeting minutes.
This series contains four bound volumes of financial journals generated by the Collins Realty Company, a real estate development business headquartered in Helena, Montana.
This series contains a daily transaction ledger generated by the Collins Company, a holding company for Collins family business interests and located in Helena, Montana.
This series consists of one bound volume containing the Beartooth Stock Company's articles of incorporation and stockholder meeting minutes. The company was a livestock raising and agricultural development business headquartered in Helena, Montana.
This collection contains the personal and professional papers of Dr. Jessie Bierman, a pioneer in the field of child and maternal health, from all stages of her life. The collection contents begin with writings related to Bierman’s family history. The collection contains the memoirs of Jessie Bierman’s father, Henry, and her writings about him.
Items from her early days include a poetry scrapbook filled with correspondence and poems, her high school yearbooks, and diploma. Her student days at The University of Montana are represented by a scrapbook and diploma. Materials from her medical career in California include a set of house call diaries, 1931-1935. A scrapbook and related correspondence represent her years with the Montana Department of Health. Her positions in government and with international missions related to maternal and child health are reflected in correspondence and other writings. The collection contains the writings and publications Bierman completed while at the University of California and elsewhere, including the study she prepared regarding the children of Kauai, Hawaii.
The collection contains the numerous honors and awards Bierman received toward the end of her career. It also contains clippings about Bierman and other clippings and publications she collected on child and maternal health issues. The photographs in the collection are from all stages of Bierman’s life. Of particular interest are the family photographs, which include photographs of the Kalispell Meat Market that was owned and operated by Henry Bierman, and photographs of Henry and Alice Bierman.
The collection includes the 119-page manuscript of Violet Dunbar's unpublished book, Homesteaders in Montana.
The collection contains information from 1957-1975. Most of the information was written by or sent to Eugene Pike. There is general information about MEC including correspondence, history, and technical and financial reports. The collection includes numerous training manuals from the Rural Electric Administration. There is information about co-operative utilities in Montana, the Pacific Northwest and the United States. There is detailed information regarding power generation in the northwest including nuclear, hydroelectric and hydrothermal.
Consists of correspondence between the MEC and other electric administration entities, including the Bonneville Power Administration, Rural Electric Administration, and other co-ops.
This series is composed of newspaper and magazine clippings collected by the MEC. Most concern territorial integrity and other matters specific to Montana.
Consists of three certificates presented to the MEC from civic entities in Missoula like the Missoula County Fair Association.
This series contains MEC rate schedules and copies of the company's report, A Look to the Past and a Plan for the Future.
Composed of statements of Eugene Pike, mostly before the Montana state legislature on territorial integrity.
This series represents the MEC's involvement with state and federal legislation governing electric generation and delivery. It includes information on the MEC's lobby for Paradise Dam and numerous folders of materials on territorial integrity.
Materials in this series were produced by the NRECA and include reports and workshop materials.
Consists of training materials made available to co-ops like the MEC.
Consists of reports, policy manuals, newsletter, and records of meetings.
This series contains financial and legal information, numerous publications, reports, and speeches.
These materials are largely reports concerning the construction and feasibility of breeder reactors, but also include some legal agreements.
This series consists of materials from Northwest Intertie, an organization in which the MEC was apparently involved. Contents include records of meetings, articles of incorporation, and reports.
This series contains extensive information on the WPPSS, particularly on its Hanford-area operations in southeastern Washington state and on its dealings with the Bonneville Power Administration. Includes reports, financial information, legal agreements.
This series consists largely of meeting minutes from the PPC, but also includes legal materials and reports.
This series contains legal materials and a report from the Federal Power Commission.
This series contains meeting records, reports, and a speech associated with the NPPA.
This series contains two reports from the MPC, on rates and its annual statistical report.
Consists of the Montana Rural Electric News 1958-1975.
This series contains materials from the Columbia Storage Power Exchange, the Committee for Paradise Dam, the Edison Electric Institute, the Mecklenberg Electric Cooperative, member services, Oregonians for Nuclear Safeguards, the State of Montana, Washington State University, and Westinghouse. The Washington State University materials are concerned with the Power Professorship and with a program for students in which the MEC provided some internship opportunities. There is also a Reports and Rate schedule.
The Missoula Mercantile Company Holiday Party Collection includes fourteen photographs and a program for the variety show entertainment show at the company's holiday party on January 6, 1945.
This collection, the Max S. Baucus Papers, represents the material sum of Max Baucus’s political career, from his work at the Montana Constitutional Convention to his appointment as the United States Ambassador to China. It includes manuscript materials, artifacts and memorabilia, microfilm, audiovisual material, photographs, and electronic records produced throughout Baucus’s time in the Montana House of Representatives, the United States House of Representatives, the Senate, and ambassadorial service. These records document policy and legislation development, constituent services, Baucus’s committee service, political campaigns, his membership in the Democratic Party, and office administration. Particularly well-represented are Senate legislative materials, as well as constituent correspondence from his time in both the House and the Senate.
The collection also includes some material from Baucus’s personal life which provides a small snapshot of his daily and social activities throughout his time in public service. This material primarily takes the form of photographs, ephemera, miscellaneous correspondence and holiday cards, invitations, and notebooks.
Materials in this series provide insight into the personal and family history of Max Baucus. They largely pertain to Baucus’s life outside of politics, though his personal and political lives inevitably overlapped as a career elected official.
This series is divided into two sub-series: Personal and Electronic Files.
This subseries represents the physical portion of this series, and contains invitations and materials related to various social events on Capitol Hill which Baucus attended, including Presidential inaugurations and White House events. It also contains a number of appointment books and wallet diaries that provide a window into Baucus’s personal schedule, in contrast to official scheduling material produced by staffers. A nearly complete run of Baucus family Christmas cards is also included, several of which feature artwork created by Baucus’s second wife, Wanda Minge.
Correspondence found within this series is largely comprised of letters marked ‘Administration-Personal’ by office staff, though there are letters relating to specific incidents in Baucus’s life. The series also includes personal notes and notebooks kept by Baucus throughout his career, containing notes and observations taken during meetings, hearings, and other events, reflections on various topical issues, draft ideas for speeches, and campaign strategies. Many of these are undated. The series likewise contains a number of writings and publications authored by Baucus, including op-eds and two peer-reviewed papers. Also notable is a pocket Constitution given to Baucus by Senator Robert Byrd in 1995, which he carried with him throughout the remainder of his Senate career.
While largely comprised of material focusing on Baucus’s personal life, the series does contain some records relating to his family—chiefly obituaries following the death of Baucus’s stepfather, John Baucus, and correspondence between Jean Baucus and Ruth Chambers. Chambers was the sister of Max Baucus’s biological father, Stephen Enke, and the correspondence took place between Jean Baucus and Chambers following Enke’s death in 1974. The series also contains a Baucus family tree and family recipes.
This subseries contains essays and op-eds authored by Baucus, as well as a 1983 New York City Marathon athlete profile sheet. Files have been renamed for clarity by the processing archivist.
This series contains material relating to Max Baucus’s time in the Montana House of Representatives. It includes press files, bills which were sponsored or cosponsored by Baucus, voting records, and subject files. Subject files constitute the majority of this series, and consist of documents created and kept by Baucus and his staff during the course of their legislative work. Materials contained within subject files include correspondence, research materials, memos, notes, reports, and other related items reflecting the pertinent political and legislative issues of the time. Staffers assigned subjects according to a topical index. This index helped to organize many administrative functions, including legislative research, the tracking of constituent opinion, the compilation of Baucus’s legislative achievements, and responses to constituent concerns.
This series contains administrative records from Max Baucus’s time in the U.S. House of Representatives. Collectively they provide a picture of the day-to-day operations within Baucus’s Washington, D.C. and state offices, reflecting the wide array of activities which take place within Congressional offices.
This subseries contains materials relating to trip planning, office equipment and supply purchases, parking, management of office finances, staff training and management, and administrative correspondence. Also included are successful academy appointments, administrative correspondence, and staff files generated by Baucus’s first Chief of Staff, Steve Browning, who held the position from 1975-1982.
This subseries is comprised of schedules outlining Baucus’s daily agenda in great detail, with to-the-minute times, locations, and background information. These files reflect the busy day-to-day schedule of a member of Congress, as well as the level of work and planning required by staffers to keep office operations running smoothly.
This subseries contains scanned versions of office administration correspondence from Baucus’s second term in the U.S. House of Representatives, largely centering on event scheduling, White House tours, and thank-you notes. These files were originally labeled incorrectly as memos and located elsewhere in the collection, and have since been concentrated in one folder. Original file names have been retained.
This series contains press files from Max Baucus’s time in the U.S. House of Representatives.
This subseries contains advertising circulars, documents from radio appearances and talks, and grant announcements.
This subseries contains press clips and official press releases.
This subseries contains public remarks and addresses given by Max Baucus while serving as a U.S. Representative. In the case of untitled speeches, file names describe the event or group to which Baucus spoke.
This subseries contains press clips and speeches. While it is likely that the majority of these files are reflected in the manuscript portion of this series, exact correlations have not been fully established. The following files constitute only a portion of the files contained within this subseries. A full list of files is available upon request.
This series contains constituent correspondence sent to Max Baucus during his time in the U.S. House of Representatives. Reflecting a variety of topical and legislative issues of the day, these letters provide a broad overview of Montanans’ concerns in mid- to late 1970s America. Environment and agriculture were two primary topics of correspondence, with many letters focusing on grazing, agricultural subsidies, proposed legislation, and wilderness. Central to environmental issues at the time was the passage of the Montana Wilderness Study Act (S. 393), introduced by Senator Lee Metcalf, which became law in 1977 and was to have profound effects on Montana’s land and its residents. Other common areas of concern included Panama Canal negotiations, postal matters, the Equal Rights Amendment, gun control, labor-related legislation, and the Milwaukee Railroad bankruptcy. The majority of files are arranged alphabetically by subject, which were assigned in accordance with an indexing system utilized in Baucus’s office to organize, code and track constituent mail and other documents. This series does not contain a name file.
Also contained within this series are form letters, mass mailings, and yellow files. Form letters were drafted by staffers in response to issues which generated large quantities of constituent mail—these summarized Baucus’s position and eliminated the need to create a unique response for each constituent. Mass mailings are mailings, often in the form of newsletters, sent to constituents on a regular or semi-regular basis. These provided updates on pending legislation, Baucus’s legislative and political activities, information on upcoming events, and sometimes included questionnaires and surveys. Yellow files consist of responses to constituents printed on yellow carbon paper, which are arranged alphabetically by constituent last name. This series also includes 9 reels of microfilm, containing scans of constituent correspondence from 1976.
This series contains legislative materials relating to Max Baucus’s time in the U.S. House of Representatives, which present a clear view of the legislative process from start to finish. These files outline the scope and extent of Baucus’s legislative work throughout his first two terms in Congress, generated and gathered by both Baucus and his staffers. Such work included policy research and development, correspondence and collaboration with members of Congress and other stakeholders, Committee hearings and deliberations, gathering and collating data, and drafting and editing legislation.
This subseries includes bills and resolutions which Baucus sponsored or co-sponsored.
This subseries contains floor statements, prepared remarks delivered by Baucus on the House floor.
This subseries contains material profiling Baucus’s collaborative and individual legislative work and viewpoints. It contains legislative correspondence, typically consisting of correspondence between Baucus and other members of Congress intended to generate support for or opposition to legislation, initiatives, and issues. VIP correspondence—correspondence with well-known or prominent figures—is also included, as well as correspondence with the standing U.S. President. Also included are hearing statements, prepared remarks delivered by Baucus in Committee hearings, and voting records from the 94th and 95th Congresses.
This subseries contains materials generated by Baucus’s Committee service during his time in the House of Representatives. Baucus served on the House Committee on Appropriations for the entirety of his two terms in the House, and the majority of materials contained within this subseries relate to Committee work undertaken by Baucus and his staffers relating to investigations, hearings, legislation, nominations, and other projects. Also included in this subseries are committee assignment materials and files relating to the Committee for a More Active and Effective Congress, a liberal political action committee founded by Eleanor Roosevelt.
This subseries is comprised of records which outline all aspects of legislative research, development, and implementation. File types include, but are not limited to, memos, correspondence, reports, publications, legal briefs, invitations, and legislative drafts. Subject files give a clear indication of the predominant issues which directed Baucus and his staffers’ work. Rural issues—particularly post offices and rural health—ranked high on the list, in addition to President Carter’s zero-based budgeting mechanism for government planning.
This subseries contains scanned Dear Colleague letters, floor statements, hearing statements, and memos. The analog versions of these documents, along with many other types of documents produced by Baucus’s office, were scanned by staffers and converted to digital formats for storage in the office’s document management system, OnBase. Many OnBase files were duplicated or scattered during the transfer process from Baucus’s office to the University of Montana, and the majority of the files in this subseries have subsequently been rearranged by category and renamed to clarify their content, due to a lack of adequate original description. Dear Colleague letters and memos files reflect only a small portion of their analog counterparts, whereas floor and hearing statements files represent a near-complete replication of their analog counterparts. While it is likely that many of the files in this subseries are reflected in the manuscript portion of this series, exact correlations have not been fully established.
This series contains administrative records from Max Baucus’s time in the U.S. Senate, relating specifically to Washington, D.C. and district office operations. Such records reveal the wide range of activities that regularly occur in a Congressional office, particularly those related to legislative research, scheduling, and handling the large volume of constituent mail. They also point to the high turnover of legislative staffers and the fast pace of legislative activity, as well as the subsequent need for detailed training materials and a high level of communication.
This subseries contains materials reflecting the administrative capacity of a congressional office, and include a number of academy appointments, requests for appointment to military service academies that were ultimately successful. These files are not exhaustive; records of academy appointees are maintained by the academies themselves. Also contained within this subseries are a run of ‘Thank you Good Letters’, letters sent to Max Baucus expressing appreciation for speaking engagements, constituent assistance, support of various issues, and other services provided. Such letters were identified and retained by staffers for campaign and legislative record purposes. Friendly File cards likewise record constituents willing—or potentially willing—to assist in fundraising, canvassing, volunteering, and other supportive efforts. Additional administrative files include mail reports, indexes and filing keys, conference-planning materials, memos, invitations, staff training manuals and guides, and trip files. In addition to scheduling materials, trip files contain a myriad of documents including memos, background information on people and places, and drafts of prepared remarks. Of particular note in this subseries are those records pertaining to the Montana Economic Development Summit, a recurring event focusing on Montana jobs and the economy established by Max Baucus in 2000.
This subseries, while similarly reflecting the administrative functions of a Congressional office, is comprised of schedules and related documents which outline Baucus’s daily agendas. These documents provide great insight into the day-to-day activities of a member of Congress, as well as the high level of planning and meticulous attention to detail required by staffers to plan activities and maintain adherence to scheduled activities. They include specific times, locations, transportation information, background information on both issues and people, and other useful contextual information.
This subseries contains born-digital administrative records from Baucus’s time in the Senate, records which were created in an electronic format. These files act as a foil to analog administrative records, serving as the digital footprint of administrative functions within Baucus’s office. In addition to such day-to-day administrative material as calendars, forms, call reports, and manuals, this subseries also contains indexes of paper records sent to National Records Centers in Denver, Colorado and Suitland, Maryland for preservation. Also included are inventories of records which were created in preparation for their shipment to the University of Montana.
Files from Baucus’s Senate website, www.baucus.senate.gov, have not been retained. Archived iterations of the website between October 13, 1999 and April 4, 2014 can be found on the Internet Archive’s WayBack Machine at https://web.archive.org/web/*/http://www.baucus.senate.gov/.
This series contains press materials produced during Max Baucus’s time in the U.S. Senate, providing insights into his communication style, public persona, and representation in local and national press. They also reveal the ways in which Baucus connected with his constituents, highlighting prioritized topical issues and demographics.
Staffers regularly collected news clippings that mentioned or featured Max Baucus, and those contained within this subseries document the entirety of his career in the Senate. Scattered throughout are Max Baucus Reports, short legislative updates and topical pieces that were written by Baucus and published in various Montana newspapers between 1979 and 1984.
This subseries consists of prepared statements released by Max Baucus regarding legislation, current events, sponsored and cosponsored bills, and other political and legislative issues of the day. It also contains news headlines and summaries from Montana newspapers, which were filed with some early office press releases. Beginning in March of 1993 press releases were filed individually, with topics typed on individual folder labels. While press releases are listed here by date, a list of these topics is available upon request.
It is possible to trace Max Baucus’s growing national exposure and expertise on particular topics through this complete run of speeches and public remarks given during his time in the Senate. Baucus gave speeches to some groups on a regular basis, casting light on one of the ways in which he maintained connections with important components of his base. These included groups and events like the Montana Graingrowers Association, the AFL-CIO, the Montana Cattlemen’s Association, the Wool Growers Convention, the Montana Democratic Party Mansfield-Metcalf Dinner, and high school and college commencements. We can also trace his growing proficiency in Asian foreign affairs through the many speeches relating to Vietnam, Korea, Japan, and especially China, focusing particularly on trade, economic and human rights issues. For those speeches which are untitled, file names describe the group(s) to which Baucus spoke.
This subseries contains preparatory and administrative press-related materials, which include memos, interview preparations, and an indexed directory to important speeches.
This subseries contains press clips, press releases, and speeches spanning Baucus’s Senate career. Speeches and some press releases have been renamed to clarify file content. While it is likely that the majority of these files are reflected in the manuscript portion of this series, exact correlations have not been fully established. The following speech files constitute only a portion of the files contained within this subseries. A full list of files is available upon request.
This series contains constituent correspondence generated and received throughout Max Baucus’s time in the U.S. Senate. Consisting of correspondence generated by constituents, staffers, and Baucus himself, the material in this series weaves a 35-year narrative of constituent concerns, pressing topical issues, support for and opposition to legislation, and, more broadly, the symbiotic relationship between Senator Baucus and his constituents.
Constituent Correspondence contains correspondence between Montana constituents and Max Baucus’s offices. Congressional offices undergo high staff turnover, resulting in ever-evolving records management systems and organizational schema. These evolutions are commonly reflected in constituent correspondence management, given the large volume of material, and are also affected by changes in technology. Such was the case in Baucus’s Congressional offices, particularly with the advent of in-house Senate computing technology in the mid-1980s. Constituent correspondence organizational schemas have thus been pieced together from office manuals, filing guides and computer code lists. This subseries does not contain a name file.
Several unique categories of correspondence can be found in the first fifteen boxes: ‘Dear Montana’, Form Letters, Mass Mailings, and Robo Letters. There is no clear distinction between ‘Dear Montana’ correspondence and other constituent correspondence. Form Letters consist of letters drafted by staffers in response to issues which had generated large quantities of constituent mail, summarizing Baucus’s position and eliminating the need to craft a unique response to each constituent. Similarly, Mass Mailings are mailings--often in the form of newsletters--sent to constituents on a regular or semi-regular basis. These provided updates on pending legislation, Baucus’s legislative and political activities, information on upcoming events, and sometimes included questionnaires and surveys. All of these groupings are arranged chronologically, with the exception of Robo Letters. Robo letters, which date from 1980-1985, are letters generated by an early word-processing machine known as the “ROBO” system. They consist of carbon copy letters sent from Baucus to constituents, responding to inquiries and concerns. Robo Letters from 1980 are arranged in alphabetical order by constituent last name; letters from 1981-1985 are arranged chronologically.
As is evident in the folder titles, in-office methods of filing constituent correspondence changed over the years. From 1979-1987, correspondence is arranged by topics designated by an in-house filing guide and computer code list. From 1987 through 1998, correspondence is arranged by unique numerical identifiers assigned to each letter as it was entered into the office’s Constituent Services System (CSS). These numbers do not necessarily connote, however, exact chronological order, and the dates assigned to these folders often correspond to the date on which correspondence was entered into the CSS, rather than the date on which a letter was written.
The second subseries, Petitions, contains mass mailings sent to Max Baucus between 2010 and 2014. These petitions center on a number of legislative and topical issues and were mailed by constituents to convey collective grievances, opinions, and support for/opposition to particular pieces of legislation. Petitions have been sampled to reduce volume—original quantities and sampling data are available upon request.
The third subseries consists of 799 individual reels of microfilm, containing filmed images of constituent correspondence and mail reports generated between 1979 and 1988. Please ask the archivist for a list of individual reel contents.
This subseries consists of electronic files related to constituent services, including e-newsletters, mass mailings, scanned correspondence and microfilm, and a smattering of in-office mail reports exported from Microsoft Outlook dating from 2011-2013. While much of the scanned correspondence does likely have analog surrogates, it is not wholly representative of analog constituent correspondence held within the Max S. Baucus Papers. Similarly, the microfilm files in this subseries correspond to a portion of physical microfilm reels, but not all. This subseries also contains data files exported from Constituent Services Systems (CSS), large-scale databases that manage the relationship between a member of Congress and their constituency. These systems facilitated a variety of activities related to constituent relations, including scheduling, correspondence and casework management, and document management. Baucus’s offices utilized a proprietary program called Intranet Quorum (IQ) from the mid-1990s to 2008, and VOICE from 2008 to 2014.
This series contains material relating to Max Baucus’s legislative work in the U.S. Senate, encompassing policy research and development, legislation sponsorship and co-sponsorship, Senate votes, correspondence on legislative matters, and committee work. It provides a comprehensive overview of Baucus’s voting habits, relationships with his colleagues in Congress and other significant stakeholders, legislative priorities, and leadership roles in the Senate.
This subseries contains the text of bills and resolutions sponsored or cosponsored by Baucus, and do not constitute a complete run. All sponsored and cosponsored bills can be found online at the U.S. Congress’ website. Legislative Activities are federal compendiums of all sponsored and cosponsored bills but include summaries of the bills rather than the full text.
This subseries contains floor statements, remarks given by Baucus on the Senate floor. While floor statements are listed here by Congress, they are also foldered individually by statement topic. Please ask an archivist for an inventoried list of individual floor statement topics.
The Congressional Profile contains material which, as the title implies, outlines the scope and nature of Baucus’s legislative work while serving in the Senate. It includes hearing statements, “Dear Colleague” and other legislative correspondence, briefing books, and legislative strategy materials. “Dear Colleague” correspondence traditionally consists of letters sent to and from other members of Congress requesting support for or against legislation, explaining policy stances, or urging other legislative actions. The “Dear Colleague” correspondence in this collection does consist primarily of such letters, though this category also contains other, less formalized correspondence between members of Congress, including handwritten and signed notes and letters on official letterhead. “Dear Official/VIP” is a Baucus office designation, applied to correspondence to and from federal officials, ambassadors, executives, and other designated important administrators. “Dear President” letters consist of letters sent from Baucus, and sometimes colleagues, to the sitting president requesting support or urging specific action on executive matters.
Legislative Strategy material contains files laying out legislative plans and strategies for upcoming Congresses, legislation, or issues. Briefing books are materials gathered by staffers to prepare Max Baucus for various trips, public appearances, hearings, meetings, and other events. They contain background information regarding people he would be meeting or speaking with, pieces of legislation, companies and cities, answers to tough questions he might encounter, and any other necessary preparatory material.
Voting records were produced by four distinct entities: the Congressional Research Service, the Democratic Policy Committee, various interest groups, and Baucus’s office itself. While no one grouping contains a complete run of Baucus’s Senate voting record, together they provide a comprehensive overview of his voting habits and record. Whereas materials produced by both the Congressional Research Service and the Democratic Policy Committee provide a fairly straightforward, quantitative record of Baucus’s Senate votes, those generated by interest groups and Baucus staffers tend to be more interpretative. Interest group voting records examined how Baucus voted in regards to particular issues, and were often a good gauge of support in various topical areas. Some voting records produced by Baucus staffers also focused on particular issues, though they served a variety of other purposes: tracking Baucus’s support (via votes) of particular constituencies, bolstering campaign claims, and analyzing his overall legislative record for future planning purposes. The most recent document was authored in 2018 by a former staff member, Spencer Gray, and provides a comprehensive summary of Baucus’s legislative accomplishments.
Baucus’s service on various congressional committees and subcommittees during his time in the Senate is the basis for the third subseries, Committee Files. While official congressional committee records must remain in the care of the committee itself, the congressional office records contained within this subseries were generated during work undertaken by Baucus and his staffers relating to committee investigations, hearings, legislation, nominations, and other projects. Significant within this subseries are committee records generated during Baucus’s tenure as Chairman of the Senate Committee on Finance, in addition to Senate Committee on Environment and Public Works records regarding the asbestos environmental disaster in Libby, Montana. Baucus served as Chairman of the Senate Committee on Finance on three separate occasions: January 3, 2001-January 20, 2001; June 6, 2001-November 22, 2002; and January 4, 2007-February 6, 2014. He also served as Chairman of the Committee on Environment and Public Works from January 3, 1993-December 1, 1994.
Subject files consist of materials generated by Baucus and his staffers during the course of legislative and administrative work. These records, filed by subject, present perhaps the clearest view of legislative work in Congress, outlining all aspects of legislative research, development and implementation. They also illuminate the complex web of relations on Capitol Hill, outlining the ways in which Baucus and his staff worked together as well as with other members of Congress, Congressional staffers, federal employees, public officials, and constituents. Given the level of detailed description and arrangement, this subseries serves as a useful tool with which to both trace trending topical issues of the day and determine legislative priorities for Baucus and his staffers. Trade, agricultural, and environmental issues are consistently significant, as are postal services and rural health. File types include, but are not limited to, memos, correspondence, reports, publications, legal briefs, invitations, white papers, and legislative drafts.
Libby files are comprised of materials pertaining to the asbestos-based environmental disaster which came to light in Libby, Montana in the late 1990s. Max Baucus was instrumental in advocating for the rights of Libby citizens affected by the asbestos crisis, and pushed for the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) to declare a Public Health Emergency in Libby, the first time that the agency ever did so. He also cosponsored several pieces of legislation related to asbestos injury compensation and environmental cleanup. This subseries includes material related to this legislation as well as the 2005 Department of Justice criminal proceedings against W.R. Grace and Company, W.R. Grace’s 2007 bankruptcy proceedings, asbestos litigation reform, the EPA investigation, Libby’s Superfund designation, and asbestos-related health programs and initiatives.
Documents generated by staffers in the course of their work as legislative assistants, legislative correspondents, mailroom specialists, and other legislative and administrative roles in Max Baucus’s offices constitute the eighth subseries, Staff Files. These materials focus primarily on particular topical subjects and legislation, correlating with staffers’ area(s) of expertise. While most materials were generated in the course of a singular staffer’s work, some were created by multiple staffers as indicated by initial inventories accompanying the files.
This subseries consists of electronic records documenting all aspects of legislative work conducted by Max Baucus and his staffers throughout his time in the Senate. It contains briefing books, unofficial committee files, floor statements, hearing statements, legislative correspondence, memos, and a small selection of sponsored and cosponsored bills, staff files, and subject files. Of particular note are Senate Committee on Finance materials, as Baucus was Chairman and/or ranking member for 12 of the 35 total years he served on the Committee. These materials consist of Finance Chairman letters, press clips, press releases, and hearing statements. Files also include hearing statements from Baucus’s other committee work, memos, subject files, voting record materials, and a number of staff files. Some files and folders have been renamed to better reflect their contents. While it is likely that many of these files are reflected in the manuscript portion of this series, exact correlations have not been fully established.
This series contains material relating to Max Baucus’s political campaigns, shining a light on the complexity of Congressional campaign and polling activities.
These files were generated during Max Baucus’s first campaign for public office in 1972, when he ran for a seat in the Montana House of Representatives. They contain candidate statements and press materials, in addition to some campaign ephemera.
This subseries is comprised of materials from Max Baucus’s two House campaigns in 1974 and 1976, as well as files on campaign opponents and other candidates. Files include campaign press materials, circulars and brochures, correspondence and memos.
Materials from Max Baucus’s six campaigns for election to the Senate constitute the third subseries. The groundwork for Baucus’s political campaigns was often laid two years in advance, and involved a great number of campaign staff, volunteers, canvassers, fundraisers, and political consultants. Files include campaign press materials, circulars and brochures, memos, debate preparatory materials, voter surveys and polls, endorsements, election results, and campaign ephemera.
Materials in this subseries consist of audio and video recordings documenting Baucus’s House and Senate campaigns. Contents include campaign advertisements, radio spots, interviews, inauguration activities, campaign events, and debates. A great deal of the magnetic tape and film reels have little to no accompanying description, and largely appear to contain short audio and video clips recorded for use in campaign advertisements. Particularly noteworthy in this subseries are Baucus family videos from 1955, presumably utilized for campaign ads.
This subseries contains results from Max Baucus’s 2000 campaign primaries, as well as campaign files from Max Baucus’s Senate run in 2002, his fifth Senatorial campaign. These files include campaign media files, website material, fliers, the opening statement for a July 2002 debate with opponents Mike Taylor, Stan Jones, and Bob Kelleher, and notes from Taylor’s withdrawal press conference on October 10, 2002. Some files have been renamed to better reflect their contents.
This series consist of materials documenting Max Baucus’s participation in local and national political initiatives, largely based around his membership in the Democratic Party. Files relate to Baucus’s earliest participation in the political process—the 1972 Montana Constitutional Convention—as well as his involvement in various Democratic Party groups and activities, including the Democratic Policy Committee and the Democratic Study Group.
This subseries represents the physical portion of this series, and contains records relating to Baucus’s participation in local and national political processes.
This subseries contains a 1972 press clip quoting Max Baucus while he was serving in the Montana Constitutional Convention. It also contains a number of topical “Talking Points” broadsides disseminated by the Democratic Policy Committee on April 10, 2003.
This series contains photographic material documenting both Max Baucus’s personal life and political career. This series contains photographic material documenting both Max Baucus’s personal life and political career.
This subseries is comprised of physical photographs and slides spanning the entirety of Max Baucus’s political career, in addition to some family and personal photographs. These images document all aspects of Baucus’s tenure as an elected official, including legislative work, campaign activities, administrative duties, Democratic Party events, and public appearances. Many titles encompass multiple images, generally because multiple photographs were taken at a single event or because numerous photographs were taken on a single day. Original Baucus staff-generated titles have been retained.
This subseries contains miscellaneous photographs included in a new accession (group of materials transferred to the repository at a single time) which were transferred to the repository in 2017. These photographs largely document Baucus’s personal life and cover a wide span of time, ranging from his college days to the 2008 Mansfield-Metcalf Dinner. Photographs were assigned unique identifiers by the processing archivist.
This subseries similarly contains a number of photographs included in a new accession from 2018. It consists of images from various campaign and workday events, showing Baucus interacting and speaking with people who are likely constituents. Little descriptive information accompanied these photographs, and most are undated. Photographs were assigned unique identifiers by the processing archivist.
Photographic negatives span Baucus’s personal and political lives, each bearing a unique numerical identifier assigned by Baucus staffers. While there is no documentation directly linking these negatives to analog surrogates in this series, it is highly likely that the majority of them do correlate with photographs in Subseries 1.
This subseries consists of digital images documenting Baucus’s personal and political lives, many of which correspond with analog photographs contained within Subseries 1. While it is likely that the majority of these files are reflected in the manuscript portion of this series, exact correlations have not been fully established.
Materials in this sub-subseries consist of digital images from Baucus's tenure in the U.S. House of Representatives. These photographs were digitized by Baucus staffers prior to their transfer to the University of Montana. Original file and folder names have been retained.
This sub-subseries contains digital images taken and compiled by both Baucus staffers and Senate staffers throughout the duration of Baucus’s tenure in the U.S. Senate. Photos taken prior to Baucus’s Senate career were gathered and used by Baucus staffers for various campaign and web content purposes. Original file and folder names have been retained. The digital images that are listed constitute only a small selection of the files contained within this sub-subseries. A complete list of files is available upon request.
Materials in this sub-subseries consist of photographs transferred to the repository in 2018 on optical media. These images were subsequently converted to a digital format by the processing archivist. They document a 2011 hike in Big Sky, Montana, a CODEL (Congressional Delegation) trip to Iraq in 2007, and trade trips to the United Arab Emirates and Vietnam in 2008. Original file and folder names have been retained.
This series consists of audiovisual material, both audio and video recordings. It covers the entirety of Baucus’s congressional career, particularly emphasizing campaign material, television appearances, and Committee hearings, and reflects more than three decades of continually-evolving audiovisual technology.
This subseries contains audio recordings which document field hearings, speeches, town hall meetings, interviews, news clips, campaign appearances, floor statements, and committee hearings in which Baucus took part. Topics range widely, and many of the audio recordings were recorded in towns in and around Montana. Campaign audio in this subseries is not limited to Max Baucus’s campaigns, and also covers the campaigns of some political opponents and allies, including Pat Williams and Allen Kolstad. Most, but not all, audio recordings include Baucus—some were generated during town hall meetings and field hearings at which Baucus was not present. All audiocassettes in this subseries were digitized by Baucus’s office, files which can be found in Subseries 3, Electronic Files.
This subseries contains video recordings consisting of a variety of formats. Content includes committee hearings (including Environment and Public Works, Appropriations, Finance, and Agriculture), floor and hearing statements, speeches, interviews, public service announcements, town halls, news clips and talk show appearances, and campaign debates, ads, and statements. A variety of topics are covered, including foreign relations with Japan, Cambodia, and the Middle East, transportation, wilderness, tax reform, trade, and the asbestos crisis in Libby. Items with a VHS number have accompanying CD or DVD surrogates and were converted to digital files by Baucus staffers, which can be found in Subseries 3, Electronic Files.
This subseries contains digital audio and video recordings generated throughout Baucus’s political career. The majority of these recordings were converted from analog to digital formats by Baucus staffers, and consist of campaign advertisements, television debates, television and radio interviews, public service announcements, televised floor statements, and advertisements for events like the Montana Economic Development Summit and the Faith Community Impact Summit. Converted Optical Media consists of optical media items (CDs and DVDs) which were converted to more stable digital formats by archives staff, and an inventory of their contents is available upon request. The majority of original file and folder names have been retained, though some files have been renamed for clarity. While it is likely that many of these files are reflected in the analog portion of this series, exact correlations have not been fully established.
This series contains a wide variety of awards and other memorabilia given to Baucus in recognition of his legislative and public service work. Items include framed workday photographs, ceremonial pens commemorating the signing of legislation, framed pieces of legislation, various awards and certificates reflecting Baucus’s lengthy running career, miscellaneous artworks, and campaign ephemera.
This subseries contains the physical artifacts which make up the analog portion of this series. Items within the original accession (acquisition) were surveyed, and selected artifacts were retained based on their historic, intrinsic, exhibit, and/or research value.
This subseries contains digital images of all awards originally contained within the Baucus Papers, including artifacts which were not retained. Original file names have been retained.
This series consists of digital records from Max Baucus's tenure as the United States Ambassador to the People's Republic of China. They include nomination endorsements from Montana leaders, Baucus's nomination statement to the Senate Foreign Relations Committee, photographs from his swearing-in ceremony, a video of Baucus encouraging Chinese companies to do business in the United States, and photographs from his time spent in China as Ambassador.
The Montana Gerontology Society collection includes records related to the business of the Society, newsletters published by the organization, and materials related to its annual conferences.
This series contains administrative documents and photographs related to the Montana Gerontology Society. Documents include minutes and by-laws. Photographs are of board members.
This series contains materials related to the Montana Gerontology Society's annual conferences and is divided into three sub-series: Conference Materials, Schedules and Photographs.
This collection contains research from The Missoula Women for Peace Oral History Project, including interview transcripts, photographs and slides of MWP members and events, digital files, and organizational materials created during Dawn Walsh’s administration of the project. These resources offer a history of the creation and continued work of Missoula Women for Peace as well as an intimate look into the personal histories and activist actions of eleven group members.
The Frank D. Brown Family Papers include Frank D. Brown's business correspondence, a few personal letters, financial records, insurance registers, Society of Montana Pioneers records and correspondence, his historical, fiction, and speech writings, photos, and memorabilia. Series II, James M. Brown, includes personal correspondence and letters related to the Sons and Daughters of Montana Pioneers. Series III, Rutledge and Albertina Brown Parker, includes correspondence with Sons and Daughters of Montana Pioneers, insurance and legal issues.
Series I, Frank D. Brown, is divided into six subseries reflecting the type or theme of the materials included. Subseries 1 includes correspondence about his mining work and partnerships, a small amount of personal correspondence mostly with his daughter, Albertina (Tina) Brown, and letters related to his activities and interest in the Society of Montana Pioneers. Brown corresponded frequently with James A. Murray, Butte banker, investor and uncle of James E. Murray; Paul A. Fusz, mine owner; C.D. McClure, one of the owners of the Granite Mountain and Bi-Metallic Mines; and W.A. Clark, Montana copper baron. Subseries 2 includes financial records relevant to Brown's mining businesses, reports on various mines, insurance registers for his Philipsburg, Montana, based insurance company, and a few legal documents. Subseries 3 reflects his long time membership in the Society of Montana Pioneers and his efforts to establish historical monuments and records of significant events in Montana history. It also includes a copy of the 1865 receipt that served as proof of Brown's arrival in Montana. Subseries 4 holds Brown's historical writings about Montana, his fiction about forest animals and also stories set in foreign locales, and speeches about Montana history. Subseries 5 includes photos of the Brown Family, the Society of Montana Pioneers, friends and dignatories as well as Philipsburg and Helena locations. Subseries 6 includes biographical information, news clippings about Brown, Montana pioneers, and Montana history, a few pieces about the United Confederate Veterans, and memorabilia.
Series II, James M. Brown, includes personal correspondence and correspondence related to the Sons and Daughters of Montana Pioneers.
Series III, Rutledge and Albertina Brown Parker, includes correspondence related to Sons and Daughters of Montana Pioneers, insurance and legal issues.
The collection consists of three typescript narrative reminisces by Mitchell; the original handwritten copy for one is also present. “When Just a Pup” tells the story of his early childhood. “The Era of the Cowpuncher” gives more details of the work that he and his father did and gives comparisons between the lives of the early cowboys and the lives of those in the late 1960s. “The Steer That Wouldn’t Go to Market” tells the story of a stubborn cow with which he worked.
This collection includes Mary Birch’s narrative of the events and emotions surrounding her Montana Human Rights Complaint and her colleagues’ District Court discrimination suit. The collection contains correspondence and initial documentation gathered and prepared for the Discrimination Grievance Committee that preceded the Montana Human Rights Complaint, the Committee Findings, The Montana Human Rights Complaint and Findings, the District Court Case and Findings, and memos and news clippings related to gender equity at The University of Montana.
The collection contains clippings, correspondence, notes, photographs, publications, slides, audio material, and visual material related to Carling Malouf’s work and activities in the University of Montana’s Anthropology Department, as well as Malouf’s research on Native American tribes of the Northwest and the Plains. Materials include papers related to the history of the University of Montana’s Department of Anthropology; the history of Kyi-Yo (spelled Kyiyo since 2015), the University of Montana’s Native American Student Association; and the Native American Studies Department at the University of Montana. The collection also contains materials related to the AIM/Wounded Knee incident and ensuing trial, research on Native American Education, materials related to Montana archaeology digs and sites, and photographs and notes related to the tribes of Montana. Also included are papers, correspondence, and notes related to Carling Malouf’s involvement in the development of the Head Start program, as well as correspondence, notes, newsletters, and publications related to the Montana Archaeological Society.
This collection has been divided into five series: Series I: University Materials (1954-2004), contains notes, departmental budget information, intradepartmental correspondence, clippings, and publications related to Carling Malouf’s role in the Department of Anthropology and the Department of Native American Studies at the University of Montana. Series II: Subject Files (1891-2006), contains materials related to Carling Maoulf’s research and interests in archaeology, anthropology, and the Native American tribes of Montana. The series contains notes, correspondence, clippings, and publications. The series also contains photographs and negatives, most of which were not taken by Malouf, but were collections he considered relevant to his research. Series III: Audio Materials, (ca. 1950-2005 and n.d.), contains reel-to-reel audio and audiocassettes, A portion of this series is recorded talks and lectures of Carling Malouf, and recordings of the songs and stories of Native American tribes of the Northwest. Another portion consists of copies of prerecorded materials related to the Native Americans in the southwest. Series IV: Slides, (1939-1975 and n.d.), consists of slides and a slide index. Some of these images were taken by Carling Malouf and are arranged by tribe name or by the name of a site of archaeological interest. Others are likely commercial slides used in the classroom. In 2012 all slides were removed from carousels and boxes and placed in sleeves. Series V: Moving Image Material, was added in 2012 because of a large addition of 8mm films to the collection. The series also includes DVDs and VHS cassettes.
Notes, departmental budget information, intradepartmental correspondence, clippings, and publications related to Carling Malouf’s role in the Department of Anthropology and the Department of Native American Studies at the University of Montana.
Materials related to Carling Malouf’s research and interests in archaeology, anthropology, and the Native American Tribes of Montana. The series contains notes, correspondence, clippings, and publications. The series also contains photographs and negatives, most of which were not taken by Malouf but were collections he considered relevant to his research. The series has been arranged alphabetically by folder title. The content in the folder titled 'Helping Indians' has been restricted for privacy reasons.
Contains reel-to-reel audio and audiocassettes. A portion of this series is recorded talks and lectures of Carling Malouf, and recordings of the songs and stories of Native American tribes of the Northwest. Another portion consists of copies of prerecorded materials related to the Native Americans in the southwest.
This series contains slides. Boxes 60 and 61 of this collection, including the slide index, are reproductions of slides in other boxes of the collection. These reproductions were created by Anthropology Professor G. G. Weix in 2004 from original slides held by Carling Malouf. Weix donated these slides to the Archives prior to the acquisition of the original Malouf Slides. The reminder of the slides were donated to the Archives by the Malouf family. Some of these images were taken by Carling Malouf, others are commercial images likely used by him in the classroom. In 2012 slides originally received in slide carousels were transferred to clear sleeves to enhance preservation and access.
This series consists of moving image material filmed by or collected by Carling Malouf. Most of the titles assigned are as given by Malouf (when the material was labeled.) In 2021, films were viewed and content descriptions provided when possible. Commercial films were removed from the series in 2021.
This collection contains a scrapbook compiled by J. Clinton Rule during his time at the State University of Montana (now called the University of Montana) in Missoula. He acquired the nickname "Rusty" from his fraternity brothers and it is prominently displayed on many of the items. The scrapbook includes dance cards, ticket stubs, newspaper clippings, telegrams, photographs, correspondence, various published items from Rule's time on campus, and a freshman beanie. The collection also contains some materials relating to Rule's younger brother, Ted Rule, and his time as a part of the State University's football team.
The collection contains a chronology of Fort Missoula events, correspondence regarding the decommissioning of Fort Missoula, legal documents, maps, writings, clippings and photographs of Fort Missoula and Fort Harrison. In addition, there are documents pertaining to the Maclay business.
This series contains a chronology, reports, histories, legal documents, correspondence and clippings concerning the history of Fort Missoula and individuals stationed there.
Correspondence, legal documents, maps, and clippings concerned with or from the time surrounding the decommissioning of Fort Missoula. Much of the correspondence is with U.S. Congressman Mike Mansfield.
Contains correspondence, financial records, clippings, and an Edward Gleim Maclay biography.
Contains photographs mainly of Fort Missoula and military personnel as well as Fort Harrison in Helena, Montana.
Assembled in the mid-1970's by Bonner School Superintendent Jack L. Demmons, this collection of approximately 1,600 photographs depicts life in and around Bonner, Montana, from the late 1800s through the 1960s. Images and descriptive labels were affixed to photoboards for public display. The photoboards and labels were scanned in sections in 2007 with the goal of providing online access to the images.
The collection consists of outgoing correspondence and financial records from the law firm. Correspondence is mostly with other attorneys in and outside Missoula on real estate dealings. Financial records document general finances of the firm, and also contain two volumes of property for sale in and around Missoula.
This series contains outgoing correspondence in letterpress books. The correspondence, arranged chronologically, ranges from mundane exchanges with office suppliers, to more substantive correspondence on behalf of clients. Exchanges are mostly with other attorneys in and out of Montana.
This series contains financial records from the law firm. Most concern the day to day business of collecting fees and dispersing them to the partners. Many of the records show an active continuing interest in Missoula real estate, particularly south of the Clark Fork River, and dealings with the South Missoula Land Company. Two volumes of records of property for sale list sales in and outside Missoula, giving quite detailed descriptions of land, timber, and other factors, and indicate dealings with and for Hiram Knowles and the Missoula Land Improvement Company.
This collection includes both bound and unbound correspondence, construction and expense reports, financial ledgers, right-of-way descriptions and maps, activity logs, publicity materials (including photographs and film reels), and personnel records created by employees of the Northern Pacific Railway Company and its subsidiary lines between the years 1870 and 1968--though materials are predominantly from the late 1880s through the early 1940s.
The Northern Pacific Railway Records present an extensive and detailed resource for researching railroad corporate management structures, land ownership histories for railroad associated properties (particularly in North Dakota, Montana, Idaho, and Washington state), economic impacts created by railroad construction and repair, corporate investment into raw material production for cheap construction supplies (particularly timber), and NPRR marketing strategies spanning a period of dramatic social and economic change in the American West. Correspondence records in this collection provide significant insights into prevailing railroad management structures during the late 19th and early 20th Centuries--when the rail industry dominated the social, economic, and political realms of rural American life. As was common during the time period, NPRR field operation managers were required to be technical experts as well as administrators. The records generated and/or compiled by the railroad's engineering staff reveal this complex balance of designing construction projects, scheduling traffic flows, and managing day-to-day operations. Rights-of-way documents, property maps, and construction drawings form a uniquely valuable resource for researching individual real estate histories and large-scale settlement patterns. The financial records in this collection provide detailed accounts of occupations and pay schedules for railroad personnel, the corporation's purchases of construction materials and sundry supplies (often specific to individual stations), and freight volumes carried from the region to national markets. The NPRR promotional department records in this collection preserve sometimes overt and often subtle techniques intended to both create and reinforce the railroad's preferred images of life in the Rocky Mountain West and Pacific Northwest. These promotional materials form a particularly interesting body of evidence for comparison and contrast with perceptions presented by contemporary regional literatures as well as the department's focus on highlighting agricultural abundance during periods of drought and farm foreclosures.
Materials in this collection were predominantly generated or compiled by engineering and promotions personnel. For the most part, engineering department recording procedures are consistent; however, the materials in sub-group 2 were generated by subsidiary and associated rail lines that reflect widely varying recording policies and project goals. Additionally, NPRR engineers tended to move frequently, causing some degree of discontinuity.
This subgroup contains materials generated by employees of the Engineering Department of the Northern Pacific Railway's primary rail lines. Series III through XX contain records generated by the geographic divisions the railway established for hierarchical management (largely defined by state borders and more localized spurs). Series I and II contain records related to constructing and managing the primary lines spanning these geographic divisions. Materials primarily consist of records generated or compiled by NPRR Engineering Department managers (usually identified as "chief engineer," "assistant engineer," or "engineer in charge") regarding property acquisitions, construction projects, repair work, station activities, line traffic, production numbers from subsidiary operations, and general management.
These materials provide significant primary resources for examining railroad corporate management structures, land ownership histories for properties associated with the main railroad lines and spurs, regional and localized economic impacts created by railroad construction and repair, and cycles of railroad investment into timber harvesting as a source for cheap construction materials. Information in this series is primarily presented through general correspondence, bound financial ledgers, compiled tracking reports and narrative summaries, blue print diagrams and maps, and day books maintained by unidentified railroad staff. Throughout this subgroup the phrase "Force Reports" in folder titles refers to reports filed by line engineers regarding actual and projected personnel hours (work force) for construction and repair projects.
This series includes materials related to constructing and managing the NPRR's primary rail line, with particular attention to the Montana and Idaho legs. This series is further divided into nine subseries highlighting some of the most intensive construction projects--such as line changes, tunnels, and re-gradings. Construction planning correspondence, cost estimates, material requisitions, progress summaries, work force reports, billing records, rights-of-way documents, and design diagrams predominate the series. These materials form an extensive record of the railroad's contributions to and impacts upon the region over nearly 100 years. Construction records provide detailed accounts of local and imported supplies, work force numbers and pay schedules, large- and small-scale landscape transformations, and land ownership patterns.
This series also contains a few daily logs of station activities and line traffic, providing insights into the railroad's role within rural communities. Additionally, the series includes several folders of collected speeches and writings. None of the speeches in this series are attributed to an author and they appear to be intended for delivery to local civic groups. Most speeches are accompanied by newspaper articles and pamphlets--apparently research sources. The speeches and writings sequence includes a few brief histories of the Northern Pacific line and the communities along its routes. These histories are written from the perspective of the Northern Pacific (such that the history of a place tends to begin when the railroad arrived) and appear to be intended as light reading for passengers on cross-country trips. The speeches and writings sequence also includes foreign language promotional materials--mostly Eastern European languages--promoting the agricultural abundance of lands along the rail line.
This series includes materials related to constructing and managing the NPRR's second main rail line. As with Series I, materials in this series exhibit particular attention to the Montana leg of the line; however, they also include records from the Minnesota and Washington state legs. This series is further divided into five subseries--the largest of which details construction projects and traffic reports for the leg between Garrison and Missoula, Montana. Survey records, construction planning correspondence, cost estimates, material requisitions, progress summaries, work force reports, billing records, rights-of-way documents, and design diagrams predominate the series. Construction records detail local and imported supplies as well as work force numbers and pay schedules. Correspondence records provide insights into the challenges engineers faced in designing multiple river crossings due to narrow canyons, procedures and rationales for subcontracting work projects, and long-term operations problems (such as derailments and mud/rock slides).
Subseries 2, regarding the Garrison to Missoula leg, includes a notable sequence of correspondence, encroachment agreements, and construction designs intended to mitigate the proximity of Northern Pacific Railroad and Milwaukee Railroad lines through narrow stretches of the Clark Fork River valley, particularly near Bearmouth and Clinton, Montana.
This series consists entirely of bound accounting records maintained by the Main Line Office in Helena, Montana. These records are statewide expense summaries presented through an account balance format--as opposed to the project-by-project budgets present in the reports compiled by field staff.
This series includes bound correspondence and financial records generated by the Chief Engineers of the Idaho Division of the Northern Pacific. Financial records include detailed reports regarding construction material costs, production numbers from the Ellisport (Idaho) lumber mill, passenger manifests, and station activities--particularly from Wallace, Idaho. These latter records evidence the economic prominence of Wallace in the Idaho panhandle prior to the devasting forest fires of 1910 that burned much of the town and transformed the region's economic patterns.
This series consists of correspondence and financial records generated by the Clarks Fork and Pend d’Oreille Division, operating in western Montana and northern Idaho. Correspondence materials primarily describe line repair projects and traffic summaries. Financial records primarily detail production numbers and shipments (incoming and outgoing) for the NPRR's lumber yard in Hope, Idaho. Processed lumber from this yard was a significant source for the railroad's construction projects throughout the region. The absence of records after 1910 may reflect the impact of the 1910 forest fires that heavily impacted the region.
This series consists of correspondence, financial, and personnel records generated by the Missoula Division, operating in western Montana and northern Idaho. Correspondence materials primarily describe line construction and repair projects as well as traffic summaries. Construction records (both correspondence and financial journals) detail local and imported supplies as well as project progress reports. Personnel records in this series provide insights into the buying habits of railroad employees who used payroll vouchers to purchase goods from the company (stations and dining cars). In addition, this series includes a file detailing next-of-kin information for employees at the Drummond, Montana, station and may serve as a valuable resource for genealogical studies and local history surveys.
This series consists of construction correspondence and financial records generated by the Missouri Division of the NPRR line, operating primarily in eastern Montana. Correspondence materials primarily describe line construction and repair projects. Construction records detail local and imported supplies as well as project progress reports. This series includes progress journals that provide brief insights into work conditions and procedural routines at a few construction sites.
This series contains one bound volume of clothing, equipment, and food supply requests from the Assistant Engineer stationed in Harlowton, Montana--a post within the NPRR's Montana Division.
This series consists of correspondence, financial, personnel, and construction records generated by the Rocky Mountain Division of the NPRR line. The Rocky Mountain Division spanned most of Montana and this series includes several distinct and extensive sequences of materials documenting construction projects, management of multiple branch lines, construction blueprints, internal and outgoing correspondence, and station histories. Construction records (both correspondence and financial journals) detail local and imported supplies as well as project progress reports. Many contruction records in this series also include blueprint details for site preparation and design. Branch lines records include Boulder, Bozeman, Camp Creek, Couer d'Alene, Phillipsburg, Red Bluff, and Ruby Valley. This series contains extensive blueprints for the branch lines listed above. In addition to the branch line sequences, this series includes extensive records regarding redesign and repair of the Missoula to Garrison leg.
Internal correspondence records address construction projects, company policies, and acquisition procedures. Outgoing correspondence records predominantly detail rights-of-way inquiries, land acquisition negotiations, grazing leases for railroad properties, and freight negotiations with clients. This sequence (organized by the community or rail line closest to the recipient) presents extensive documentation of land ownership patterns, grazing patterns, and agricultural production numbers. Often clients compiled several years of production numbers into a single report to bolster their requests for freight rate adjustments.
